            leaderboard: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([320, 100], [[300, 50], [320, 50]])
                .addSize([740, 100], [728, 90])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202304265')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        // googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-mobileleaderboard')

        //     .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-1')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-1')
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-2')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-2')
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

            // PERICLES-17135
            googletag.pubads().disableInitialLoad();

        googletag.enableServices();

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="710f698b253f4a72a05065262848e275"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="710f698b253f4a72a05065262848e275">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6c4a697e-9ecf-487d-8550-03822cc12ab9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '75B2976BCE3590E4B09348480DBFB3C5';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();
    </script>

               <p>Laboratory of Advanced Materials and Energy Electrochemistry, Institute of New Carbon Materials, College of Materials Science and Engineering, Taiyuan University of Technology, Taiyuan, 030024 China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d126352152" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Bismuth (Bi) has attracted attention as a promising anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its suitable potential and high theoretical capacity. However, the large volumetric changes during cycling leads to severe degradation of electrochemical performance and limits its practical application. Herein, Bi nanoflowers are encapsulated in N-doped carbon frameworks to construct a novel Bi@NC composite via a facile solvothermal method and carbonization strategy. The well-designed composite structure endows the Bi@NC with uniformly dispersed Bi nanoflowers to alleviate the attenuation while the N-doped carbon frameworks improve the conductivity and ion transport of the whole electrode. As for sodium-ion half-cell, the electrode exhibits a high specific capacity (384.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and excellent rate performance (341.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and the capacity&nbsp;retention rate still remains at 94.9% after 5000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the assembled full-cell with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and Bi@NC anode can deliver a high capacity of 251.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and its capacity attenuates only 0.009% in each cycle after 2000 times at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work offers a convenient, low-cost, and eco-friendliness approach for high-performance electrodes in the field of sodium ion electrochemical storage technology.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304265-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304265-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are an excellent energy storage device that has been successfully applied to electric facilities and electronic equipment. However, the global storage of lithium resources is very limited and unevenly distributed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0001_R_d126352089e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The huge annual consumption (≈850 thousands Ton/year) cannot maintain human demand and the high price of lithium (≈30 thousands dollar/Ton) limits its booming development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0002_R_d126352089e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a potential substitute for LIBs, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been attracted extensive attention in recent years due to the widespread availability and affordability of sodium. Moreover, sodium and lithium elements are in the same alkaline group of the periodic table and both of them exhibit similar energy storage mechanisms and physicochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0003_R_d126352089e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of low-cost electrode materials for SIBs with high capacity and stable cycle life is crucial for massive energy storage application. However, the larger atomic radius of sodium (1.02&nbsp;Å) than lithium (0.76&nbsp;Å) causes a slow diffusion kinetics in the electrode material. Moreover, the huge volume expansion and structural damage of the anode electrode material also occur during the charging and discharging process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0004_R_d126352089e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy Carbon Energy Nano-Micro Lett.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the cycle life of the battery declines rapidly and the long-term stability is relatively poor, which is the biggest technic obstacle to the development of SIBs. Thus, it is imperative to design the ideal electrode with suitable structure and chemical composition to meet the requirements of anode for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In the past decades, a variety of materials have been investigated and tried to be served as anodes for the application of SIBs, such as carbonaceous composites (i.e., carbon nanowires, hard carbon microtubes, and camphor-derived hard carbon),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0005_R_d126352089e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin. Electrochim. Acta">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal oxides (i.e., Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MO<sub>2</sub>, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>NiO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoO<sub>2</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0006_R_d126352089e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal sulfides (i.e., MoS<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0007_R_d126352089e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev. Mater. Technol.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and other materials (i.e., MXene composites and pitch derived carbon).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0008_R_d126352089e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nanoscale Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these alternatives, carbonaceous composites have been considered as an attractive candidate to the SIBs in virtue of the low cost and remarkable reversible capacity. For example, Wang and co-workers synthesized N-doped carbon nanofiber films (NCFs) as anodes for SIBs, and the NCFs exhibited a capacity of 345&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0009_R_d126352089e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Energy Chem.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hao et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0010_R_d126352089e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared N-doped porous carbon nanosheets using coal tar pitch as a precursor, which exhibited a capacity of 144&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over 10 000 cycles. Wu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0011_R_d126352089e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized a kind of carbon nanosheets from petroleum asphalt, revealing a reversible capacity of 300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Although carbon materials have attained a certain of achievements, the limited theoretical capacity (≈279&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and low energy density (≈280&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;L<sup>−1</sup>) are insurmountable issues for the further development of large-scale energy storage systems. Nowadays, periodic table group V elements (i.e., Bi and Sb) have been attracted much attention due to their reversible reaction abilities with sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0012_R_d126352089e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. - Asian J.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, elemental Bi possesses high gravimetric (≈385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and volumetric capacities (≈3800&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>) as anode for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0013_R_d126352089e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the reversible reaction between Bi and Na (Bi ↔ NaBi ↔ Na<sub>3</sub>Bi), it can deliver a high theoretical capacity of 385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a low reaction voltage of ≈0.6&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0014_R_d126352089e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Qiu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0015_R_d126352089e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> developed a bismuth nanospheres and porous carbon composite (Bi─NS@C). As the anode for SIBs, it has a reversible discharge capacity of 106&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Liang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0016_R_d126352089e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated a 3D framework structure with carbon coated Bi nanospheres. As anode for SIBs, the composite manifested reversible capacities of 325&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 305&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Nevertheless, Bi-based anode still suffers from severe cycle degradation in the long cycle range, which is mainly due to the reasons, including volume changes during charge/discharge and its poor electronic and ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0017_R_d126352089e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small Nano Energy J. Ind. Eng. Chem. Electrochim. Acta">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is necessary to further optimize the structure of Bi-based materials to improve their electrochemical performance for SIBs. For the design of novel anode materials, several aspects should be involved, such as high electronic conductivity, abundant ion transport pathways, and excellent physicochemical stability. In general, Bi nanoparticles can expose a large number of active sites to enhance the capacity and dynamic performance of the batteries. However, their high surface energy can cause severe aggregation of materials, resulting in volume changes and unsatisfactory conductivity of electrodes. Carbonaceous composites can generally suppress the volume expansion change during charge/discharge process, while nitrogen-doped carbon can improve the conductivity and reaction kinetics of the electrode. However, there are few reports on the novel structure of Bi/N-doped carbon composites till now. Therefore, designing and constructing a composite material with continuous conductive N-doped carbon framework to anchor evenly dispersed Bi nanostructures will effectively deliver their advantages.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we developed a novel composite anode with large amounts of Bi nanoflowers uniformly encapsulated in N-doped carbon frameworks (Bi@NC) via a facile solvothermal method and carbonization strategy. On the one hand, the N-doped carbon frameworks can effectively facilitate the uniform dispersion of Bi nanoflowers to alleviate the attenuation during the circulation&nbsp;process. On the other hand, the constructed Bi@NC presents a porous framework structure which can both improve the conductivity and maintain the stability of the whole electrode. The well-designed composite structure and synergistic effect between embedded Bi nanoflowers and surrounded N-doped carbon framework endow fast kinetics of sodium ion, as well as diminish the volume expansion in Bi@NC anode during the charge/discharge process. As a result, when worked as an anode for sodium-ion half-cell, the Bi@NC electrode displayed a high specific capacity of 384.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent rate capability of 341.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a large current of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After long-term cycling for 5000 times at a high current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the electrode can still retain a capacity of 324.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (the capacity retention reaches 94.9%). Meanwhile, the assembled full-cell with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and Bi@NC anode can deliver a remarkable capacity of 251.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and its capacity only attenuates 0.009% in each cycle after 2000 times at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a facile and low-cost strategy for the construction of Bi nanoflower embedded N-doped carbon composite anodes for high-performance SIBs. Meanwhile, it put forward a new way for the green and high value-added utilization of coal tar pitch in the field of energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304265-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304265-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304265-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202304265-sec-0030-title">2.1 Physicochemical Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The schematic diagram of the preparation process of Bi@NC composites is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0001">1</a>. The mixture of PVP, CH<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>S and Bi (NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O is dissolved in EG solution and uniformly dispersed. After the solvothermal reaction in the polytetrafluoroethylene reactor at high temperature, black powdery Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoflowers are generated. The as-obtained bismuth sulfide (Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>) is added to DMF solution with CTP dissolved and dispersed by ultrasonic wave. Meantime, ultrafine NaCl is also added to construct carbon frameworks. Eventually, under the synergistic effect of ammonia and hydrogen gas during high-temperature, a composite comprising defect-rich N-doped porous carbon frameworks and uniformly embedded Bi nanoflowers is constructed. During this process, the sulfur element in Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is completely eliminated by carbothermal reduction and hydrogen atmosphere. Therefore, Bi exists in the form of simple substance in the Bi@NC composite. Moreover, after filtration and drying, the NaCl salt templates can be recycled again.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ffadbff-c5e8-4cdb-b714-34269976bec7/smll202304265-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ffadbff-c5e8-4cdb-b714-34269976bec7/smll202304265-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/22b98e3c-9b56-4789-8f99-a62e964bf084/smll202304265-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ffadbff-c5e8-4cdb-b714-34269976bec7/smll202304265-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the preparation process for the Bi@NC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphology and microstructure of the as-obtained Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Bi@NC were characterized by SEM and TEM in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2a</a>, the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> precursor presents a typical radial nanoflowers appearance with a diameter of ≈1&nbsp;µm. The petals of the nanoflowers exhibit a uniform size scale of tens of nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2b</a>). Moreover, the resultant radial nanoflower structure with a coarse surface topography is beneficial to expand the specific surface area, as well as expose more active sites to facilitate the reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0018_R_d126352089e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. - Asian J.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the assistance of NaCl salt template, the carbonization of CTP contributes to the N-doped carbon framework with quantities of large pores. Due to the reduction effect of carbon and hydrogen toward Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> precursor at high temperatures, the reduced metal Bi nanoflowers are uniformly dispersed and anchored in the carbon framework, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2c</a>. A magnified observation of a single Bi nanoflower reveals that it is tightly wrapped by the carbon framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2d</a>). In the reduction process from Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to Bi, it is obvious the radial nanoflower appearance is roughly maintained. The unique flower-like structure will guarantee both the full contact with the electrolyte and exposure of more active sites for sodium ions interaction. The N-doped carbon frameworks anchored with quantities of uniform Bi nanoflowers help to improve the structural stability of composite materials and enhances charge transfer of the sodium ion battery. The typical composite structure encapsulating radial Bi nanoflowers into porous N-doped carbon framework undoubtedly facilitates the reaction kinetics between Na ions and Bi active sites so as to alleviate the loss of irreversible capacity. According to a typical TEM image of Bi nanoflower (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2e</a>), the Bi nanoflower have a diameter of ≈1&nbsp;µm, which is consistent well with the SEM observation in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2d</a>. As observed, there are many pure bismuth spherical nanoparticles on and around the nanoflowers. It is because during the carbonization synthesis process of the composite materials, some needle-like bismuth at the edges will melt and separate to form spherical bismuth nanoparticles. In the lattice resolved HRTEM image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2f</a>, the fringes of Bi correspond to the crystal plane of (012) and there are some enlarged crystal plane stripes of carbon (<i>d</i> = 0.39&nbsp;nm) in the neighboring regions. Meanwhile, there exists clear boundaries between bismuth crystals and surrounded carbon in Bi@NC. The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2f</a>) presents typical ring-like features, being indexed to the (012), (110), and (214) crystal planes of Bi.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0019_R_d126352089e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It verifies the well-defined nanocrystalline nature of the resultant Bi nanoflowers. Furthermore, the corresponding energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0002">2g–j</a>) confirms the uniform distribution of C and N across the carbon framework and intense Bi element in embedded nanoflower region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7bfd00e0-6aa3-4b7f-83a0-044be04bf781/smll202304265-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7bfd00e0-6aa3-4b7f-83a0-044be04bf781/smll202304265-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4448e78b-e6ff-491b-ad3d-286c26ce3e91/smll202304265-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7bfd00e0-6aa3-4b7f-83a0-044be04bf781/smll202304265-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructure characterization of the samples. a) Low and b) high magnification SEM images of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> precursor. c) Low and d) high magnification SEM images of Bi@NC sample. e) TEM and f) HRTEM image of Bi@NC sample. g–j) The corresponding element mapping of C, N, and Bi.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0003">3a</a>, the XRD pattern of Bi@NC composite is similar to that of pure Bi nanocrystals and both of them are in accord with the standard card of Bi (JCPDS No. 04-007-5336). No diffraction peaks of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are detected, indicating the total transformation of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> precursor to Bi nanocrystals by carbothermal and hydrogen at high temperature. In addition, there exists a broad peak at ≈24° in the Bi@NC pattern, which corroborates the existence of amorphous carbon. The weak peaks located at 30.2° (−212) and 32.8° (130) indicate the presence of a small amount of bismuth oxide. Raman spectra of Bi@NC and pure Bi are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0003">3b</a>. The characteristic peak at 310&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is assigned to Bi nanocrystals. Meanwhile, two characteristic peaks at ≈1343 and 1592&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> for Bi@NC are attributed to the D- and G-bands of carbon, respectively. The ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is calculated to 1.1, indicative of the widespread defects in the derived porous carbon framework owing to the etching effect of ammonia gas. In order to explore the mass proportion of Bi in Bi@NC composite, TGA tests were performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0003">3c</a>, the thermogravimetric curve of Bi@NC starts to rise ≈300&nbsp;°C, which is due to the weight gain caused by Bi oxidation. Subsequently, the decrease in overall weight is mainly due to the weight loss of carbonaceous materials oxidized to carbon dioxide. Finally, the reaction is completed at ≈500&nbsp;°C and the product is converted into Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. According to the 18% weight loss of Bi@NC sample, the Bi content is calculated to ≈73.4% in the Bi@NC composite. In order to identify the specific surface area and pore distribution, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption measurements were performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0003">3d</a>, the Bi@NC composite shows a typical IV type isotherm. Based on the isotherms, the Bi@NC composite is determined to have a large specific surface area of 173.5&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As a comparison, the pure Bi nanocrystals exhibit a tiny value of ≈3.2&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The pore size distribution reveals that most of pores in the Bi@NC composite are located at about &lt;1&nbsp;nm, being consistent with the widespread existence of micropores formed by the ammonia etching.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9253ac0b-b94a-4c3f-8944-3322387c179b/smll202304265-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9253ac0b-b94a-4c3f-8944-3322387c179b/smll202304265-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c98c6d1f-12a8-4d4c-8066-7b61ca786c24/smll202304265-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9253ac0b-b94a-4c3f-8944-3322387c179b/smll202304265-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of Bi@NC composite and pure Bi nanocrystals. c) Thermogravimetric curve of Bi@NC composite. d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm and pore-size distribution (insert) of the samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>XPS was used to analyze the chemical state of the samples. The survey spectra of Bi@NC are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0004">4a</a>, in which Bi, C, N, and O signals were clearly detected. No S element was monitored, indicating the whole transformation of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoflowers into Bi nanoflowers. Since the peak of bismuth oxide is not detected in XRD, it indicates that O should be derived from coal tar pitch of carbon precursor. The high-resolution C 1<i>s</i> profile of the Bi@NC can be deconvoluted into three peaks at 284.8, 285.8, and 288.2&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the C─C, C─N, and C═O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0004">4b</a>). It further proves the existence of C─N bond in Bi@NC, and the nitrogen content in the carbon framework is calculated to ≈4.8&nbsp;at.%. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0004">4c</a>, the high-resolution profile of N 1<i>s</i> can be resolved into three peaks, including quaternary-N (N-Q, 401.4&nbsp;eV), pyrrolic-N (N-5, 400.2&nbsp;eV), and pyridinic-N (N-6, 398.2&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0020_R_d126352089e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The existence of these three peaks indicates that N heteroatoms have been successfully doped into the carbon framework. In general, the N-5 were formed to five-membered aromatic rings by breaking C─C bonds and the N-6 were formed by replacing C with N at defect sites or on edges.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0021_R_d126352089e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Energy Chem. Fuel Process. Technol. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Herein, the presence of N-5 and N-6 has a positive effect on improving the conductivity of carbon framework. Meanwhile, N-5 and N-6 can also introduce defects to the carbon framework and strengthen the binding ability to bismuth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0022_R_d126352089e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0004">4d</a>, the high-resolution spectrum of Bi mainly includes two peaks for Bi─O bond located at 159.2 and 164.1&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Bi 4<i>f</i><sub>7/2</sub> and Bi 4<i>f</i><sub>5/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0023_R_d126352089e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to unavoidable oxidation during sample preparation, Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is detected by the XPS measurement.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/50e4349e-eb74-475a-8d7f-81fcc4b6dc19/smll202304265-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/50e4349e-eb74-475a-8d7f-81fcc4b6dc19/smll202304265-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e0fe598e-4fe7-4d75-ba6d-da5030bede8c/smll202304265-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/50e4349e-eb74-475a-8d7f-81fcc4b6dc19/smll202304265-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectrum of the Bi@NC composite. a) Survey spectrum. b) C 1<i>s</i>. c) N 1<i>s</i>. d) Bi 4<i>f</i>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304265-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202304265-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance of Sodium Ions Half-Cell</h3>
            
            <p>In order to evaluate the electrochemical performance of Bi@NC electrode, a CR2032 type half-cell was assembled for comprehensive assays (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5</a>). As a comparison, the half-cell with pure Bi anode was also measured. The rate performance tests were conducted in the current density range of 0.1 to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5a</a>). It is noticeable that the Bi@NC composite electrode delivers a high discharge specific capacity of 384.8, 376.1, 362.4, 354.8, 351.9, 349.3, and 341.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The detailed Bi@NC charge and discharge curves at different current densities are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). When the current density restored from 10 to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of Bi@NC electrode is recovered and close to the initial. By&nbsp;comparison, the pure Bi sample exhibits a specific capacity of 379.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the beginning, but decays rapidly to only 163.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. With continuous increasing cycle number and current density, it can merely deliver limited capacities under the same condition, indicating that the structure has been damaged and the capacity is irreversible. In addition, the morphology and structure of the electrode after charging and discharging cycles are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The nanoflowers maintain a relatively complete structure, which verifies the bismuth anode did not suffer a large physical change during the cycling process. On the whole, compared with Bi, it is obvious Bi@NC maintains more stable reversible capacity and superior rate performance. The significant superiority of Bi@NC confirms the robustness of the reaction kinetics for electrode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a95b07c-2cd4-49da-af3b-3bbd1038519a/smll202304265-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4a95b07c-2cd4-49da-af3b-3bbd1038519a/smll202304265-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58c63793-4e8a-4117-9f28-68a6980c7b7e/smll202304265-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4a95b07c-2cd4-49da-af3b-3bbd1038519a/smll202304265-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the electrode. a) Rate performance. b) GCD profiles of the Bi@NC at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) CV profiles at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Long-term cycles of Bi@NC at current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Comparison with relevant reports.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5b</a> displays the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles of Bi@NC electrode at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. For all the three cycles, it exhibits two obvious discharge platforms (0.68 and 0.49&nbsp;V) and two flat charge platforms (0.60 and 0.75&nbsp;V), corresponding to a two-step sodiation/desodiation process. In the initial cycle, the electrode exhibits the discharge capacity of 455.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and charge capacity of 387.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with the coulombic efficiency of 85.1% (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In the second and third cycles, the charge and discharge curves almost overlap, indicating the excellent reversibility of the electrode material. In contrast, the pure Bi electrode decays rapidly (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). To further investigate the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of the Bi@NC electrode, an uninterrupted in situ XRD test was carried out and the results are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). During the stage of discharge, the characteristic peak of Bi located at 27.1° gradually attenuated and faded away, while a new characteristic peak at 31.8° is formed simultaneously and enhanced gradually, which is attributed to the generation of NaBi. The weakening of NaBi peaks and the strengthening of the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi peaks occur simultaneously during deeper discharge until the disappearance of NaBi peaks, which indicates that NaBi has been completely converted to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. In the following charging process, the diffraction peak of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi gradually weakens until it disappears, which is accompanied by the appearance of NaBi. As the charging process continues, the peaks of NaBi gradually weaken and Bi eventually appear, which is related to the conversion of NaBi to Bi. From the results of in situ XRD patterns, Bi can be completely sodiated and desodiated, indicating a good reversibility of the electrode. The CV curves for Bi@NC were performed at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> for three cycles in the potential range of 0.01–1.5&nbsp;V, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5c</a>. It is observed the wide cathodic peak was located at 0.5–0.7&nbsp;V in the first cycle, which belongs to the irreversible formation of SEI film and the alloying reaction process from Bi to NaBi. As for the anodic process, the peak at 0.61&nbsp;V is attributed to the desodiation reactions of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phases to NaBi, while the peak located at 0.75&nbsp;V corresponds to the de-alloying reaction of NaBi to Bi. As the number of cycles increases (i.e., the subsequent second and third cycles), the CV curves still maintain a good repeatability. The redox peak of the Bi@NC becomes sharper and almost overlap, indicating the high reversibility of Bi@NC anode. These results are in good accordance with that of GCD result. Aside from the superior rate behavior, the long-term cyclic stability test was further performed at a large current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), with the increase of charging and discharging times, the capacity of the electrode still remains stable. After 5000 cycles of constant current charging and discharging, the capacity&nbsp;retention of Bi@NC reaches 94.9% with the coulombic efficiency of almost 100%. Compared with previous reports related to bismuth-based materials, such as Bi@3DGFs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0024_R_d126352089e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nano Bi@carbon nanofiber,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0025_R_d126352089e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Res.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Porous Bi/N─C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0026_R_d126352089e1040" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bi@C composite,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0027_R_d126352089e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bi@N─C sphere,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0028_R_d126352089e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chemistry">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bi─C/CF,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0029_R_d126352089e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0005">5e</a>, the detailed data are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the novel stable structure of Bi@NC composite endows the electrode with not only high reversible specific capacity, but also excellent rate capability at different current densities from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">CV measurements were performed from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> to investigate the diffusion process of the Bi@NC electrode, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6a</a>. As observed, the Bi@NC electrode exhibits four similar redox peaks&nbsp;at different scan rates. With the increase of scanning rate, the CV curves possess two pairs of distinct redox peaks with a similar profile and position, indicating an excellent reversibility. According to the previous reports, the relationship between the current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) of the CV curves follows the equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0014_R_d126352089e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304265-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6d58fa7b-4a27-4252-93e0-6acf146f95de/smll202304265-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304265:smll202304265-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304265-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi>
                                 <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              </mrow>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{I = a}}{{\mathrm{v}}^{\mathrm{b}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants, <i>I</i> and <i>v</i> are the current and the scan rate. The value of <i>b</i> can be calculated by fitting log (<i>v</i>)–log (<i>i</i>) plots. The value of <i>b</i> ranges from 0.5 to 1, which can reflect the capacitance behavior of electrode materials. When the <i>b</i> value approaches to 0.5, it corresponds to the diffusion-controlled process and when the <i>b</i> is closes to 1, the process is dominated by surface capacitance. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6b</a>, the fitted <i>b</i> values for oxidation and reduction reaction are 0.52, 0.57, 0.56, and 0.63, respectively. The contribution of capacitance helps to accelerate the kinetics of electrochemical reactions, which is beneficial for the high-rate performance of the Bi@NC electrode.</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202304265-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c8880c28-c489-43bc-b448-eb058044d9bd/smll202304265-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c8880c28-c489-43bc-b448-eb058044d9bd/smll202304265-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6fc75d86-d482-4752-9d4b-10cb9b287aa9/smll202304265-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c8880c28-c489-43bc-b448-eb058044d9bd/smll202304265-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304265-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304265" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of Bi@NC electrode at various scan rates. b) Log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) at different scan rates. c) EIS diagram (the inset shows the corresponding equivalent circuit). d) The relationship plots of <i>Z</i>′ versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region. e) GITT curves of Bi and Bi@NC. f) Sodium ions diffusion coefficient for Bi and Bi@NC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were performed to further evaluate the kinetics of the Bi@NC electrodes. The Nyquist plots of the Bi@NC and Bi electrodes exhibit a similar trend, including a semicircle in the high frequency and a diagonal line in the low-frequency region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6c</a>). After fitting with the equivalent circuit (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6c</a>), the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of Bi@NC is determined to ≈2.6&nbsp;Ω while that of Bi is ≈18.5&nbsp;Ω, which indicates that the N-doped carbon framework enhances the conductivity of the whole electrode and improves the diffusion rate of sodium ions. Meanwhile, the EIS results of the Bi@NC electrode after different cycles are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The stabilization of Nyquist plots manifests that the Bi@NC electrode has an excellent stability during the cycling. In general, the sodium ions diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be quantitatively analyzed according to the equations:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0014_R_d126352089e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304265-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/974078ae-06cf-4d2a-8e2b-ff1bf372422f/smll202304265-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304265:smll202304265-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304265-math-0002.png">

                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>ω</i>, <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>A</i>, <i>n</i>, <i>F</i>, and <i>C</i> are the angular frequencies, gas constant (8.314 J K<sup>−1</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup>), absolute temperature (298&nbsp;K), the surface area of the electrode, the number of electrons, Faraday constant (96500 C mol<sup>−1</sup>), and the concentration of sodium ions, respectively. According to the above formula, it is concluded that the value of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is inversely proportional to <i>σ</i>, wherein <i>σ</i> is closely related to the slope of the line <i>Z</i>′-<i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6d</a> shows the linear fitting of <i>Z</i>′<i>-ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> in the low frequency region for Bi@NC and Bi electrodes. The oblique lines depict that the <i>σ</i> value of the Bi@NC electrode is significantly smaller than that of the pure Bi electrode, revealing the faster electrode reaction kinetics of the Bi@NC. The GITT curves for Bi@NC and Bi are executed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304265-fig-0006">6e</a> (the more details are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The diffusion coefficient of sodium ions in the electrode can be obtained from the following equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304265-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304265-bib-0014_R_d126352089e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304265-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/09d7c20d-5e3f-4355-a578-e5b1cc52074b/smll202304265-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304265:smll202304265-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304265-math-0004.png">
